9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode

4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.

6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but

9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.

Now I have a thorough understanding of the problem. Let me summarize: **Architecture:** - 4 layers: [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA] (pattern "K", "K", "K", "M") - Each layer has an attention block (KDA or MLA) followed by MoE FFN (64 experts, top-8 routed + 1 shared) - Hidden dim: 2304, KDA: 32 heads × 128 dim, MLA: 32 heads, kv_lora_rank=512, qk_nope=128, qk_rope=64, v_head=128 - MoE intermediate: 1024 - Group size for quantization: 128 **Key challenge:** - All projection weights are W4A16 (int4 weights, bf16 activations) - Store as packed int4 (2 per byte along input dimension), with per-group-128 asymmetric dequant (scales + zeros) - The goal is to fuse int4 unpack + dequant + GEMV into a single kernel to avoid materializing full bf16 weights **Weight shapes to handle:** - KDA layer: - q_proj: (2304, 32*128=4096) - QuantLinear(2304, 4096) - k_proj: (2304, 4096) - v_proj: (2304, 4096) - g_proj: (2304, 4096) - o_proj: (4096, 2304) - beta_proj: Linear(2304, 32) - tiny, bf16 - MLA layer: - q_proj: (2304, 32*(128+64)=6144) - kv_a: (2304, 512+64=576) - kv_b: (512, 32*(128+128)=8192) - o_proj: (32*128=4096, 2304) - MoE layer (per block): - router: Linear(2304, 64) - tiny, bf16 - gate: QuantExperts(64, 2304, 1024) - up: QuantExperts(64, 2304, 1024) - down: QuantExperts(64, 1024, 2304) - Shared: gate, up, down (1 expert each) So there are 4 blocks, each with a MoE. The weights are: KDA (×3): - QuantLinear(2304, 4096): q, k, v, g, o → 5 per KDA = 15 - Linear(2304, 32): beta → 3 per KDA × 3 MLA (×1): - QuantLinear(2304, 6144): q_proj - QuantLinear(2304, 576): kv_a - QuantLinear(512, 8192): kv_b - QuantLinear(4096, 2304): o_proj MoE (×4): - Linear(2304, 64): router - QuantExperts(64, 2304, 1024): gate, up (= 2) - QuantExperts(64, 1024, 2304): down (= 1) - QuantExperts(1, 2304, 1024): s_gate, s_up (= 2) - QuantExperts(1, 1024, 2304): s_down (= 1) **Strategy:** 1. Write Triton kernels for fused int4 dequant-GEMV 2. For the KDA layer: fuse qkv projections, short conv, then the recurrent state update 3. For MLA: can we absorb the kv_b projection? 4. For MoE: fuse expert dequant + GEMV, batch across selected experts Let me think about the Triton kernel design: **Fused int4 dequant-GEMV kernel:** - Input: x [K] (bf16), w_q [K//2, N] (uint8), scales [K//128, N] (bf16), zeros [K//128, N] (bf16) - Output: y [N] (bf16) - Algorithm: - Load x in chunks - For each chunk, unpack int4 weights, dequant, multiply-accumulate - Never write full dequantized weight to memory Let me think about the Triton implementation more carefully. For batch-1 GEMV with int4 weights: - Weight: [K, N] in int4 → stored as [K//2, N] uint8 - We want y[n] = sum_k x[k] * w[k,n], where w[k,n] = (unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//g,n]) * scales[k//g,n] The key insight: we can process by groups and tiles. Let me design the kernel: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) # Iterate over K dimension in chunks for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): k_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) k_mask = k_offs < K # Load x values for this chunk x = tl.load(x_ptr + k_offs, mask=k_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # For each n, compute the dot product contribution # We need to unpack w_q and apply dequant for kk in range(0, BLOCK_K, 2): # Load two int4 weights packed in one byte # w_q is [K//2, N] wq_row = k_start + kk if wq_row < K // 2: wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + wq_row * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) lo = wq_byte & 0xF hi = (wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF # scale and zero index = k//group_size # Need to figure out which scale/zero group each k belongs to ``` Wait, let me think more carefully about this. The weights are packed along the input (K) dimension: w_q[k//2, n] contains w[k*2, n] in low nibble and w[k*2+1, n] in high nibble. For a GEMV y[n] = sum_k x[k] * w[k,n], we can iterate over k: - At each k, we need w[k,n] = (w_int4[k,n] - zeros[k//128,n]) * scales[k//128,n] The challenge with a Triton kernel is efficiently handling the irregular access pattern of unpacking int4 weights while doing the GEMV. Actually, let me think about this differently. A more natural approach for Triton might be: 1. Tile over the output dimension N (since we have many outputs) 2. For each tile, iterate over the K dimension 3. Within each K iteration, load the packed int4 bytes, unpack, dequant, and accumulate The key issue is that int4 packing is along K, so for a fixed N tile, we can load the packed bytes and process them sequentially. Let me think about the memory access pattern: - w_q is [K//2, N] — row-major for K//2 (packed K dimension) - For a fixed n (output neuron), the weights w[0,n], w[1,n], w[2,n], ... are stored as: - w_q[0, n]: low nibble = w[0,n], high nibble = w[1,n] - w_q[1, n]: low nibble = w[2,n], high nibble = w[3,n] - etc. So for fixed n, we read w_q[:, n] sequentially and unpack. For Triton, a good approach: - Each program handles BLOCK_N output neurons - Iterate over K in chunks of BLOCK_K - For each chunk, load x[BLOCK_K], then for each k in the chunk, load w_q[k//2, n_offset:], unpack, dequant, accumulate Actually, the most efficient approach for Triton would be to: 1. Have each program handle a block of output neurons (BLOCK_N) 2. Iterate through K with a BLOCK_K stride 3. For each K-block, load the packed int4 weights for that block (shape [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N]) 4. Unpack to int4 values 5. Dequant using the appropriate scale/zero for each group 6. Multiply by x and accumulate Let me be more precise: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N n_offs = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): # Load x chunk k_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) k_mask = k_offs < K x_chunk = tl.load(x_ptr + k_offs, mask=k_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # For each group within this K chunk # Each group has `group_size` elements along K # scales/zeros: [K//group_size, N] grp_start = k_start // group_size grp_end = (k_start + BLOCK_K - 1) // group_size + 1 # We need to handle unpacking # The packed weight for row k and column n is stored at wq_ptr[k//2 * N + n] # Low nibble: w[2*(k//2), n] = w[k & ~1, n] # High nibble: w[2*(k//2)+1, n] = w[k|1, n] # Better approach: iterate k in pairs for kk in range(0, BLOCK_K, 2): k_abs = k_start + kk if k_abs < K: k_even = k_abs & ~1 # round down to even packed_row = k_even // 2 # Load packed byte for this row and our N block wq_row = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) # Unpack w_lo = (wq_row & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_hi = ((wq_row >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Get scale and zero for this group g_idx = k_abs // group_size # But scales/zeros have shape [K//group_size, N] — we need to load for our N block # This requires indexing into the scales/zeros matrix... ``` Hmm, the scale/zero indexing is the tricky part. For a given k and n: - Group index: k // 128 - scales[group_idx, n], zeros[group_idx, n] Since we're iterating over K, we need to figure out which group each k belongs to and load the appropriate scale/zero. One approach: instead of iterating by individual k, iterate by groups. Since group_size=128, and BLOCK_K can be a multiple of group_size: ```python # Process K in chunks of GROUP_SIZE GROUP = 128 # must equal group_size for grp in range(0, K, GROUP): # Load x for this group k_offs = grp + tl.arange(0, GROUP) k_mask = k_offs < K x_grp = tl.load(x_ptr + k_offs, mask=k_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load scale and zero for this group (one per output neuron) g_idx = grp // GROUP scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_idx * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_idx * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0) # Now iterate through the packed rows within this group # Each byte contains 2 int4 weights (even and odd k) for pk in range(0, GROUP // 2): k_even = grp + 2 * pk packed_row = k_even // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) w_even = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Dequant and accumulate acc += x_grp[2*pk] * (w_even - zero) * scale if 2*pk + 1 < GROUP: acc += x_grp[2*pk + 1] * (w_odd - zero) * scale ``` Wait, but this doesn't work well with Triton's programming model because `x_grp[2*pk]` is a scalar index into a vector, and Triton's semantics... Actually, in Triton you can do this with tl.load. Let me think again. Actually, let me think about which approach gives the best memory access pattern. The weights are stored as [K//2, N] where the first dimension is the packed K dimension. For GEMV with batch=1, the output is [N]. The input x is [K]. Option A: Each program computes one output neuron. - For each k, load x[k], load w_q[k//2, n], unpack, dequant, multiply, accumulate. - This is simple but has poor memory coalescing for w_q. Option B: Each program computes BLOCK_N output neurons. - For each k (or k-block), load a vector of w_q[k//2, n:n+BLOCK_N], unpack, dequant, scale by x[k], accumulate. - Better coalescing. Option C: Each program handles a block of K and a block of N. - Load x[BLOCK_K], load w_q[BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N], do a mini-GEMV. - Best for cache utilization. I think Option C is best. Let me design it properly. For Triton, I'll use a 1D grid over N: ```python @triton.jit def int4_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N n_offs = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) # Process K in groups for grp in range(0, K, group_size): # Load scale and zero for this group (one per output neuron) g_idx = grp // group_size scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_idx * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_idx * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Process this group in sub-chunks for k_start in range(grp, min(grp + group_size, K), BLOCK_K): k_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) k_mask = (k_offs < K) & (k_offs < grp + group_size) # Load x for this sub-chunk x_chunk = tl.load(x_ptr + k_offs, mask=k_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Process packed weight rows # Each byte = 2 int4 weights # w_q has shape [K//2, N], so row k//2 for j in range(0, BLOCK_K, 2): k_abs = k_start + j if k_abs < K and k_abs < grp + group_size: packed_row = k_abs // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) w_lo = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_hi = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) acc += x_chunk[j] * (w_lo - zero) * scale if j + 1 < BLOCK_K and k_abs + 1 < K and k_abs + 1 < grp + group_size: acc += x_chunk[j + 1] * (w_hi - zero) * scale # Store result tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Wait, there's an issue. When `k_abs` is even, the next row is odd and packed in the same byte. But my inner loop increments by 2, so for each packed byte I process both the even and odd elements. But I need to check that the odd element (k_abs+1) is still within bounds. Actually, let me restructure this more cleanly. Since group_size = 128 is a good alignment, let me process one group at a time: For each group: 1. Load scale[grp_idx, n] and zero[grp_idx, n] (both length BLOCK_N) 2. Load x[grp:grp+128] (length 128) 3. For each pair (k_even, k_odd) = (grp + 2*i, grp + 2*i + 1): a. Load w_q[k_even//2, n_offs:n_offs+BLOCK_N] b. Unpack to get w[k_even, :] and w[k_odd, :] c. Dequant d. Accumulate: acc += x[k_even] * w_deq[k_even, :] + x[k_odd] * w_deq[k_odd, :] This is clean but we need to be careful about the last element if K is not divisible by 2 (it is for our case since all input dimensions are even). Let me also think about performance: with K = 2304 and BLOCK_N = 64, we'd have group_size=128. Each thread block processes 64 output neurons. Total grid = ceil(N/64). For each group (K/128 = 18 groups for hidden=2304): - Load scale[64], zero[64] (coalesced) - Load x[128] - 64 iterations of loading w_q row (64 bytes per iteration), unpack, compute This should be efficient. Memory traffic: we read w_q once (int4), scales+zeros once per group, x once. Output is N values. Actually wait, for this to be efficient, we need to make sure the inner loop over K doesn't have too much overhead. Let me think about the register pressure: we need to hold acc[BLOCK_N] (in float32), scale[BLOCK_N], zero[BLOCK_N], and loop temporaries. With BLOCK_N=64, that's 64 + 64 + 64 = 192 float32 registers for the main state, which is fine. But there's another concern: the inner loop has 128/2 = 64 iterations of loading w_q bytes, unpacking, and computing. Each iteration has a tl.load, bitwise ops, type conversions, and FMAs. This might create a lot of instructions. Alternative: process larger K blocks and do vectorized operations. Let me think... Actually, the simplest approach that maps well to Triton is to load a 2D tile of w_q and process it. Let me reconsider: ```python @triton.jit def int4_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) # Iterate over K dimension BLOCK_K_BYTES = BLOCK_K // 2 # number of bytes per packed row for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): # Load x chunk k_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) k_mask = k_offs < K x_chunk = tl.load(x_ptr + k_offs, mask=k_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # For each pair of K elements (one packed byte), we need the scale/zero # for the group that this K element belongs to for j in range(0, BLOCK_K, 2): k_abs = k_start + j if k_abs >= K: break # Load packed byte row for this k pair packed_row = k_abs // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) # Unpack w_even = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Get scale/zero for this group g_idx = k_abs // group_size # Load per-group scale and zero # But we can't easily do indexed load in Triton for each k... ``` The problem is that within a BLOCK_K chunk, different k elements might belong to different groups. This means we need different scales/zeros for different k values within the same inner loop iteration. To handle this cleanly, I think the best approach is to make BLOCK_K = group_size (128) and process one group at a time. That way, all k elements in one chunk share the same scale/zero. But K = 2304 is not always a multiple of 128... actually wait: 2304 = 128 * 18. Great, it's exactly 18 groups. And 4096 = 128 * 32. So all our input dimensions are multiples of 128. Let me design the kernel with this approach: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): """ Fused int4 dequant + GEMV: y = x @ dequant(w_q, scales, zeros) x: [K] bf16 w_q: [K//2, N] uint8 (packed int4, two per byte along K) scales: [K//group_size, N] bf16 zeros: [K//group_size, N] bf16 y: [N] bf16 """ pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) # Process one group at a time (group_size = 128) num_groups = K // group_size for g in range(num_groups): # Load scale and zero for this group scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + g * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Process this group's K dimension k_base = g * group_size for j in range(0, group_size, 2): k_even = k_base + j # Load x values for this pair x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + k_even).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + k_even + 1).to(tl.float32) # Load packed byte row packed_row = k_even // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) # Unpack w_even = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Dequant and accumulate acc += x_even * (w_even - zero) * scale acc += x_odd * (w_odd - zero) * scale # Store tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` This looks clean. Each program processes BLOCK_N output neurons and iterates through all groups and all packed rows. Performance characteristics: - Scales/zeros loaded once per group (num_groups times) - w_q loaded once (read from global memory, int4 = 1/4 the bytes) - x loaded K/BLOCK_N times (each program needs the full x vector) - Result stored once Wait, the x loads are a problem! Each program loads x[k_even] individually, and BLOCK_N programs all load the same x values. This means the x vector is loaded many times. For K=2304, N=4096, BLOCK_N=64, that's 64 programs, each loading x 2304 times = 64*2304 ≈ 147k loads of x. This is redundant! Better approach: Load x into the kernel once and share it. We could use a different grid strategy where x is loaded cooperatively. Actually, for GEMV, each program needs all of x. The alternative is to use a reduction approach: - Tile over both K and N - Each program processes BLOCK_K × BLOCK_N - Then reduce across K But this requires atomic adds or a separate reduction step. For GEMV, the standard approach in CUDA is to have each warp/thread block process a subset of N, read the full K dimension, and accumulate independently. The redundant x loads might not be terrible since x is small (2304 * 2 bytes = 4.6 KB) and fits in L1 cache. Once the first program loads x, it'll be in L2/L1 for subsequent programs. Still, we can optimize by having each program load x once and reuse it: Wait, actually for GEMV, x is only 4.6KB for hidden=2304, and it'll be in L2 cache after the first access. The redundancy isn't as bad as it seems because of caching. But for the MoE layers where the input dimension can be 1024 (even smaller, 2KB), this is definitely fine. Let me also think about using `tl.dot` for the inner product. In Triton, we can't directly use tensor core operations for int4 + bf16, but we can do the dequant in float and then use regular FMAs. The FMA throughput on Blackwell should be sufficient since we're memory-bound anyway. Actually, wait - I could potentially use a more efficient approach with Triton's block-level operations. Let me think... Actually, let me reconsider the approach. For GEMV, the standard high-performance approach in Triton is: 1. Each program handles BLOCK_M rows of the output (for regular GEMV, M=1) 2. Each program handles BLOCK_N columns of the output 3. Reduce over K using dot products For our case with M=1 (batch-1 decode), this simplifies to each program handling BLOCK_N output positions and iterating over K. The x redundancy is acceptable because: 1. x is tiny (4.6KB) and fits in L1 2. We're memory-bound on weight reads, not x reads OK, let me go with this approach. But there's one more optimization: instead of loading individual x values, I can use tl.load with a vector and index into it. In Triton, I can't easily index into a register vector, but I can use broadcasting. Let me finalize the kernel design and start coding. Actually, there's another consideration. For the MLA kv_b projection: the weight is [512, 8192]. This is a very wide matrix. With BLOCK_N=64, we'd have 128 programs. Each program iterates over K=512. But 512 is small, so this should be fast. For the MoE: we have many experts (64), and we need to compute GEMV for top-8 of them. The naive approach selects the top-8 experts, loads their weights, and does 8 GEMVs. But we can batch them more efficiently. OK, let me start implementing. I'll create: 1. A Triton kernel for fused int4 dequant-GEMV 2. A wrapper that integrates with PyTorch 3. Optimized KDA step 4. Optimized MLA step 5. Optimized MoE step For the MLA, there's an important optimization opportunity: the `kv_b` projection is applied to ALL cached KV latents, not just the new token. In the baseline, this materializes the dequantized kv_b weight (512×8192 bf16 = 16MB) and does a matmul with the growing KV cache (ctx_len × 512). For long contexts, this dominates. We need to fuse the dequant here too. But actually, for the MLA at decode time, the KV cache grows. At each step, we add one new latent to the cache, and then compute kv_b on the ENTIRE cache. This is O(ctx_len) work and will dominate at long contexts. The baseline dequants the weight once (16MB) and does the matmul. For our solution, we can: 1. Fuse the dequant into the kv_b GEMV 2. OR absorb the kv_b projection into the attention computation (MLA absorb trick) The "absorb" trick: instead of computing k_nope and v from the KV cache (which expands dim), we can absorb kv_b into the attention scores. Specifically: - k_nope = c_kv @ W_k (where W_k is part of kv_b) - v = c_kv @ W_v (where W_v is part of kv_b) - Attention: scores = q_nope @ k_nope^T, output = softmax(scores) @ v If we absorb: scores = q_nope @ (c_kv @ W_k)^T = q_nope @ W_k^T @ c_kv^T = (q_nope @ W_k^T) @ c_kv^T And output = softmax(scores) @ (c_kv @ W_v) = softmax(scores) @ c_kv @ W_v But this doesn't directly help because we still need to compute the attention output for the new token. Actually, for decode (batch-1), the attention is: - q has shape [H, qk_nope+qk_rope] for the NEW token - k_nope has shape [ctx_len, H, qk_nope] (after kv_b projection) - v has shape [ctx_len, H, v_head] (after kv_b projection) The "absorb" trick computes: - scores[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_k^T @ c_kv^T + q_rope[h] @ k_rope^T (where W_k is reshaped from kv_b) - But W_k is part of the dequantized kv_b weight... Actually, the absorb trick in FlashMLA works differently. They split kv_b into W_UK (for k_nope) and W_UV (for v). Then: - q_nope_absorbed = q_nope @ W_UK^T → this gives [H, kv_lora], which is the effective query against the raw KV cache - The attention becomes: scores = q_nope_absorbed @ c_kv^T + q_rope @ k_rope^T - Then o = attention_weights @ c_kv, followed by o @ W_UV This absorbs the "up-projection" (kv_lora → head_dim) into the query side for the first matmul, and defers the value projection to after the attention reduction. The benefit is: 1. We compute a smaller dot product (kv_lora=512 vs qk_nope=128) for the nope part 2. But we still need the value projection... Actually wait, let me reconsider. For decode, the MLA attention is: 1. q: [H, 128+64] (one token) 2. k_nope: [ctx_len, H, 128] 3. v: [ctx_len, H, 128] The contiguous score computation: scores[h] = sum_d q_nope[h,d] * k_nope[all_tokens, h, d] + sum_d q_rope[h,d] * k_rope[all_tokens, d] With absorb: - Let W_UK be the k_nope part of kv_b: [kv_lora, H * qk_nope] - Let W_UV be the v part of kv_b: [kv_lora, H * v_head] - k_nope[t, h, :] = c_kv[t, :] @ W_UK[:, h*qk_nope:(h+1)*qk_nope] - v[t, h, :] = c_kv[t, :] @ W_UV[:, h*v_head:(h+1)*v_head] Absorb W_UK into q: - q_nope_absorbed[h, :] = q_nope[h, :] @ W_UK_per_head[h]^T → [kv_lora] (where W_UK_per_head[h] is [kv_lora, qk_nope] for head h) - scores_nope[h, t] = q_nope_absorbed[h, :] @ c_kv[t, :] = sum_d q_nope_absorbed[h,d] * c_kv[t,d] This avoids materializing k_nope entirely! The attention dot product goes from dim 128 to dim 512 (kv_lora), which is larger but avoids the expensive kv_b up-projection. Then for the output: - attention_weights: [H, ctx_len] (after softmax over ctx_len) - v[t, h, :] = c_kv[t, :] @ W_UV_per_head[h] → [v_head=128] - o[h, :] = sum_t attention_weights[h, t] * v[t, h, :] = sum_t attention_weights[h, t] * (c_kv[t, :] @ W_UV[h]) = (sum_t attention_weights[h, t] * c_kv[t, :]) @ W_UV[h] = c_kv_weighted[h, :] @ W_UV[h] So we compute: 1. c_kv_weighted[h, :] = sum_t attention_weights[h, t] * c_kv[t, :] → [kv_lora=512] per head 2. o[h, :] = c_kv_weighted[h, :] @ W_UV_per_head[h] → [v_head=128] per head This is exactly the "absorb" trick! We avoid the kv_b up-projection for k_nope AND for v. We only do: - Per head: q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T → [512] (fused dequant for W_UK[h]) - Per head: c_kv_weighted[h] @ W_UV[h] → [128] (fused dequant for W_UV[h]) And the attention computation itself uses the raw kv_lora latents. This is a massive win at long context because: - Baseline: compute k_nope (ctx_len × H × 128) and v (ctx_len × H × 128) by dequantizing kv_b and doing matmul. At ctx=16384, this is 16384 × 32 × 128 × 2 ≈ 134M elements → significant bandwidth. - Absorb: compute q_nope * W_UK^T (32 × 128 × 512 = 2M ops) and attention-weighted c_kv * W_UV (32 × 512 × 128 = 2M ops). The attention itself operates on ctx_len × 512 dtype, which is larger but we avoid the explicit kv_b matmul. But wait, we still need to compute the attention scores: - scores_nope[h, t] = q_absorbed[h] @ c_kv[t] → [H, ctx_len] - scores_rope[h, t] = q_rope[h] @ k_rope[t] → [H, ctx_len] These are O(H × ctx_len × kv_lora) and O(H × ctx_len × qk_rope) respectively. With kv_lora=512, this is larger than the baseline's O(H × ctx_len × qk_nope=128). Hmm. But the key insight is: in the baseline, we FIRST dequantize kv_b (512×8192 → expensive) and THEN compute the attention. With absorb, we skip the dequant-matmul entirely. The attention computation might be more FLOPs but we save huge bandwidth. For batch-1 decode, we're bandwidth-bound, not compute-bound. So reducing memory traffic is the right trade-off. Let me think about whether the absorb trick actually saves bandwidth: Baseline bandwidth (MLA step, one token): 1. q_proj GEMV: read x[2304] bf16 + read weights int4 → small 2. kv_a GEMV: read x[2304] bf16 + read weights int4 → small 3. kv_b GEMV on ENTIRE cache: read c_kv[ctx_len, 512] bf16 + dequant kv_b[512, 8192] bf16 → LARGE 4. Attention: scores, softmax, weighted sum → moderate 5. o_proj GEMV: small With absorb: 1. q_proj GEMV: same 2. kv_a GEMV: same 3. Per-head q_absorb = q_nope @ W_UK[h]^T: 32 GEMVs of [128] × [128, 512] → actually this is small 4. Attention on raw latent: scores = q_absorb @ c_kv^T + q_rope @ k_rope^T → O(ctx_len × 512) operations with bf16 data, still bandwidth from reading c_kv and k_rope 5. c_kv_weighted = softmax @ c_kv: reads c_kv again 6. Per-head o = c_kv_weighted @ W_UV[h]: 32 GEMVs of [512] × [512, 128] → small 7. o_proj GEMV: same Step 3 avoids the kv_b dequant. But steps 4 and 5 read c_kv twice. The baseline reads c_kv once but writes k_nope+v (much larger). Actually, for the baseline: - Read c_kv [ctx_len, 512] → 16384*512*2 = 16.8 MB (at ctx=16384) - Read dequantized kv_b [512, 8192] → 4.2 MB - Write k_nope+v [ctx_len, 32, 256] → 16384*32*256*2 = 268 MB! (wow, that's huge) For absorb: - Read c_kv [ctx_len, 512] → 16.8 MB - Read c_kv again [ctx_len, 512] → 16.8 MB (for weighted sum) - Read k_rope [ctx_len, 64] → 16384*64*2 = 2.1 MB - Total reads ≈ 35.7 MB vs baseline: - Read c_kv → 16.8 MB - Read dequantized kv_b → 4.2 MB (but kv_b had to be dequantized first) - Write k_nope+v → 268 MB - Read k_nope+v for attention → 268 MB - Total memory traffic ≈ 557 MB So absorb saves ~500 MB of memory traffic at ctx=16384. This is huge! But wait, the baseline also reads back the dequantized kv_b weight. The dequant process is: read int4 weight, write bf16 weight, read bf16 weight. That's another penalty. OK, I'm convinced the absorb trick is critical for the MLA layer at long contexts. Let me implement it. Now let me also think about the MoE. For the MoE, we have: - Top-8 experts selected - For each expert: gate(2304×1024), up(2304×1024), down(1024×2304) - Plus shared expert The baseline: 1. Select top-8 experts 2. Dequant all selected experts to bf16 (read int4, write bf16) 3. Batched GEMV for gate and up 4. SiLU gating, element-wise multiply 5. Batched GEMV for down For our solution: 1. For each expert, fused dequant-GEMV for down We can process multiple experts in parallel with CUDA streams or by batching in the Triton kernel. Let me write a more careful version: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) num_groups = K // group_size for g in range(num_groups): g_idx = g scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_idx * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_idx * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0) scale_f = scale.to(tl.float32) zero_f = zero.to(tl.float32) k_base = g * group_size for j in range(0, group_size, 2): k_even = k_base + j x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + k_even).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + k_even + 1).to(tl.float32) packed_row = k_even // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) w_even = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) acc += x_even * (w_even - zero_f) * scale_f acc += x_odd * (w_odd - zero_f) * scale_f tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue with the group_size handling. To compute q_absorb[h], we need: q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T Where W_UK[h] is the k_nope part of kv_b for head h. kv_b has shape [kv_lora, H * (qk_nope + v_head)] = [512, 32*(128+128)] = [512, 8192]. For head h, W_UK[h] = kv_b[:, h*qk_nope : (h+1)*qk_nope] = [512, 128] and W_UV[h] = kv_b[:, H*qk_nope + h*v_head : H*qk_nope + (h+1)*v_head] = [512, 128] So for each head, we need to dequant a slice of the kv_b weight. We can either: 1. Dequant the entire kv_b weight and slice (expensive) 2. Actually, this gets complex. Let me think about an alternative approach for the MLA that's simpler but still has the absorb property. Alternative: Instead of per-head kv_b slices, I can do a single GEMV with the full kv_b weight, and then reshape: Step 1: q_full = q_nope_flat @ W_kv_b_nope (where W_kv_b_nope is the k_nope part of kv_b) But q_nope is [H, 128], and W_kv_b_nope is [kv_lora, H*128]. This is H GEMVs. Hmm, I could reshape q_nope to [H*128] and do q_nope_flat @ W_kv_b_nope → [kv_lora]. But that's not what we want! The per-head dot product with the KV cache requires per-head q_absorb vectors. Actually wait. Let me re-examine. The absorb trick: We want scores[h, t] = sum_{d_k} q_nope[h, d_k] * k_nope[t, h, d_k] Where k_nope[t, h, :] = c_kv[t, :] @ W_UK[h] (W_UK[h] is [kv_lora, qk_nope]) So: scores[h, t] = sum_{d_k} q_nope[h, d_k] * (sum_{d_c} c_kv[t, d_c] * W_UK[h, d_c, d_k]) = sum_{d_c} c_kv[t, d_c] * (sum_{d_k} q_nope[h, d_k] * W_UK[h, d_c, d_k]) = sum_{d_c} c_kv[t, d_c] * q_absorb[h, d_c] Where q_absorb[h, d_c] = sum_{d_k} q_nope[h, d_k] * W_UK[h, d_c, d_k] So q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T → [kv_lora] We can compute all heads at once: q_absorb_all = q_nope_reshaped @ W_UK_all^T Where q_nope_reshaped is [H, qk_nope] and W_UK_all is [H, kv_lora, qk_nope] (reshaped from kv_b's k_nope part). Actually this is just computing each head's q_absorb independently. Since there are 32 heads, we can either: 1. Launch 32 small GEMV kernels 2. Batch them into one kernel For simplicity and to avoid overhead, let me batch them. I can write a kernel that computes multiple GEMVs (one per head) in a single launch. Actually, let me step back. The cleanest approach is: ```python # For each head h: # q_absorb[h] = fused_int4_gemv(q_nope[h], wq_kv_b_nope_head[h], scales, zeros) # o_absorb[h] = fused_int4_gemv(c_kv_weighted[h], wq_kv_b_v_head[h], scales, zeros) ``` Where the kv_b weight is split into per-head slices. The weight is stored as [kv_lora//2, H*(qk_nope+v_head)] uint8. To extract head h's k_nope part: - Column range: h*qk_nope to (h+1)*qk_nope - For v_head part: H*qk_nope + h*v_head to H*qk_nope + (h+1)*v_head The fused GEMV kernel already handles column slicing via n_offs. So we can just pass the appropriate n_start offset to the kernel. But the scales and zeros are also column-major. For a given head's slice, we need the corresponding columns of scales and zeros. Since the weight has shape [kv_lora//2, H*(qk_nope+v_head)], scales has shape [kv_lora//128, H*(qk_nope+v_head)]. For head h's k_nope slice (columns h*qk_nope:(h+1)*qk_nope): - wq columns: same range - scales columns: same range - zeros columns: same range We can just pass the column pointers with offset. OK, here's my plan for the MLA absorb implementation: ```python def mla_step_absorb(self, x, st): # 1. q_nope from q_proj (fused GEMV) # 2. kv_a projection (fused GEMV), split into c_kv and k_rope # 3. RoPE on q_rope and k_rope # 4. Update KV cache # 5. Per-head q_absorb computation (fused GEMV for each head's kv_b slice) # 6. Attention scores with latent cache # 7. Softmax + weighted sum of KV latents # 8. Per-head output projection (fused GEMV for each head's kv_b v-slice) # 9. o_proj (fused GEMV) ``` This is getting complex. Let me think about what matters most for performance and correctness. For correctness, I need to match the reference exactly. The absorb trick is mathematically equivalent but uses different intermediate precision (bf16 vs fp32 in parts). I need to make sure the cosine similarity stays >= 0.98. For performance, the key wins are: 1. Fused int4 dequant-GEMV for all linear layers (saves ~4x weight bandwidth) 2. MLA absorb trick (saves kv_b dequant+matmul at long contexts) 3. Efficient MoE with fused expert dequant Let me start implementing now. I'll write the code in stages and test. Let me start with the Triton kernel.

Triton 3.6.0 and PyTorch 2.11.0+cu128 are available via `uv run`. Now let me start implementing the solution. Let me understand the key dimensions I need to handle: **KDA layer (×3):** - QuantLinear(2304, 4096): q_proj, k_proj, v_proj, g_proj, o_proj - Linear(2304, 32): beta_proj (tiny, bf16) **MLA layer (×1):** - QuantLinear(2304, 6144): q_proj - QuantLinear(2304, 576): kv_a - QuantLinear(512, 8192): kv_b - QuantLinear(4096, 2304): o_proj **MoE (×4):** - Linear(2304, 64): router (tiny, bf16) - QuantExperts(64, 2304, 1024): gate, up - QuantExperts(64, 1024, 2304): down - QuantExperts(1, 2304, 1024): s_gate, s_up - QuantExperts(1, 1024, 2304): s_down Now let me design the solution carefully. The key insight for the fused int4 GEMV kernel: we need to avoid materializing the bf16 weight matrix. The weight is stored as int4 (packed two per byte), and we need to: 1. Read the packed int4 bytes 2. Unpack to int4 values 3. Dequant using per-group scales and zeros 4. Multiply by activation and accumulate ALL in one pass without writing intermediate bf16 weights to memory. For the MLA absorb trick: - Instead of expanding the KV latent cache (ctx_len × kv_lora) to k_nope (ctx_len × H × qk_nope) and v (ctx_len × H × v_head) by applying the dequantized kv_b weight, we: 1. Absorb the k_nope projection into q: q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T 2. Compute attention on the raw latent cache: scores = q_absorb @ c_kv^T + q_rope @ k_rope^T 3. Weighted-sum the latents: c_kv_weighted = attn_weights @ c_kv 4. Defer the v projection: o[h] = c_kv_weighted[h] @ W_UV[h] This avoids the expensive [ctx_len, 512] × [512, 32*256] matmul entirely. However, this absorb trick requires per-head access to kv_b weight slices. The kv_b weight is [512, 8192] where 8192 = 32 heads × (128 nope + 128 v_head). So: - W_UK for head h: columns [h*128 : (h+1)*128] - W_UV for head h: columns [32*128 + h*128 : 32*128 + (h+1)*128] For each head, we need to do two small GEMVs (q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T and c_kv_weighted[h] @ W_UV[h]). The first is [128] @ [128, 512]^T = [128] @ [512, 128] → [512]. The second is [512] @ [512, 128] → [128]. These are all tiny operations. With 32 heads, we have 64 tiny GEMVs. The overhead of launching 64 kernels might be significant. Better to batch them. One approach: write a batched GEMV kernel that processes multiple (head, weight_slice) pairs. But this gets complicated with different weight slices pointing into the same weight buffer. Simpler approach: use a single kernel that processes the q_absorb computation for all heads at once. The kernel takes: - q_nope: [H, qk_nope] = [32, 128] - W_UK (from kv_b): we need per-head access - Output: q_absorb: [H, kv_lora] = [32, 512] For the per-head weight access, we can use pointer arithmetic to offset into the kv_b weight and scale/zero buffers. Let me think about this more carefully in the context of the fused GEMV kernel... Actually, I think the cleanest approach is: 1. Write a base `fused_int4_gemv` kernel 2. Use it from PyTorch with appropriate pointer offsets for per-head slices 3. Launch multiple instances (one per head) for the MLA absorb case Since each head's GEMV is tiny (input dims: 128 or 512), the kernel launch overhead could dominate. But with CUDA graphs or by fusing operations, we can minimize this. Alternative for MLA: what if I DON'T use the absorb trick, and instead just fuse the dequant into the kv_b matmul? The baseline dequants kv_b to bf16 (16MB) and then does the matmul. If I fuse the dequant, I: 1. Read int4 kv_b (2MB) 2. Read c_kv [ctx_len, 512] (16.8MB at ctx=16384) 3. Write k_nope+v [ctx_len, 32, 256] (268MB at ctx=16384) vs absorb: 1. Compute q_absorb: read int4 kv_b W_UK slices (part of 2MB), write [32, 512] (tiny) 2. Attention: read c_kv [ctx_len, 512] + k_rope [ctx_len, 64], compute scores [32, ctx_len] 3. Weighted sum: read c_kv again [ctx_len, 512], write [32, 512] (tiny) 4. Compute output: read int4 kv_b W_UV slices, write [32, 128] (tiny) Total memory traffic for absorb: - Read kv_b int4: ~2MB (only the per-head slices we need) - Read c_kv: 2 × 16.8MB = 33.6MB - Read k_rope: 2.1MB - Total: ~38MB For fused kv_b GEMV (no absorb): - Read kv_b int4: 2MB - Read c_kv: 16.8MB - Write k_nope+v: 268MB - Then read k_nope+v for attention: 268MB - Total: ~555MB So absorb saves ~500MB! At 1.8 TB/s bandwidth, that's ~280 μs saved just from reduced memory traffic. Given that the decode step is probably in the 1-10ms range, this is a meaningful speedup. But I'm worried about two things: 1. Precision: the absorb trick changes the order of operations and uses different intermediate representations 2. Correctness: check.py compares against reference.py, which doesn't use the absorb trick For point 2, the check compares the FINAL output and state by cosine similarity. The absorb trick is mathematically equivalent (just a different order of matrix multiplications), so the result should be the same up to floating-point precision. With cosine similarity threshold of 0.98, this should be fine. Actually, wait. Let me verify the mathematical equivalence more carefully. Reference: k_nope[t, h] = c_kv[t] @ W_UK[h] (W_UK[h] is [kv_lora, qk_nope]) v[t, h] = c_kv[t] @ W_UV[h] (W_UV[h] is [kv_lora, v_head]) score[h, t] = q_nope[h] @ k_nope[t, h] + q_rope[h] @ k_rope[t] o[h] = sum_t softmax(score[h])[t] * v[t, h] Absorb: q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T ([qk_nope] @ [qk_nope, kv_lora] → [kv_lora]) score[h, t] = q_absorb[h] @ c_kv[t] + q_rope[h] @ k_rope[t] = (q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T) @ c_kv[t] + q_rope[h] @ k_rope[t] = q_nope[h] @ (W_UK[h]^T @ c_kv[t]) + q_rope[h] @ k_rope[t] = q_nope[h] @ k_nope[t, h] + q_rope[h] @ k_rope[t] ✓ o[h] = sum_t softmax(score[h])[t] * (c_kv[t] @ W_UV[h]) = sum_t attn[h, t] * c_kv[t] @ W_UV[h] = (sum_t attn[h, t] * c_kv[t]) @ W_UV[h] (since W_UV[h] doesn't depend on t) = (attn[h] @ c_kv) @ W_UV[h] = sum_t softmax(score[h])[t] * v[t, h] ✓ Great, it's mathematically equivalent. But the numerical precision might differ: - Reference: dequant kv_b → bf16 → compute k_nope (bf16) → fp32 for attention - Absorb: dequant kv_b → bf16 for q_absorb computation, then fp32 for attention Actually, the precision issue is subtle. Let me trace through both paths: Reference (baseline using _wbf which dequants to bf16): 1. w_kv_b = dequant(kv_b_wq, kv_b_scales, kv_b_zeros) → [512, 8192] bf16 2. kvb = c_kv @ w_kv_b → [ctx_len, 8192] in bf16 3. k_nope = kvb[:, :32*128].view(ctx_len, 32, 128) → bf16, then .float() 4. v = kvb[:, 32*128:].view(ctx_len, 32, 128) → bf16, then .float() 5. scores in fp32, softmax in fp32 6. o in fp32 via einsum Absorb (my version): 1. For each head h, dequant W_UK[h] slice → fused into GEMV 2. q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T → [512] computed in fp32 with fused dequant 3. Convert q_absorb to bf16 4. scores = q_absorb @ c_kv^T → fp32 5. softmax in fp32 6. c_kv_weighted = attn @ c_kv → fp32 (or bf16) 7. For each head h, c_kv_weighted[h] @ W_UV[h] → [128] computed with fused dequant The main numerical difference is: - Reference quantizes the intermediate k_nope and v to bf16 before the fp32 attention - Absorb avoids these intermediate quantizations This should actually give BETTER precision for absorb, not worse. So correctness shouldn't be an issue. OK, let me now plan the implementation in detail: ### 1. Fused int4 dequant-GEMV kernel ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, # [K] bf16 input wq_ptr, # [K//2, N] uint8 packed int4 weights scales_ptr, # [K//128, N] bf16 per-group scales zeros_ptr, # [K//128, N] bf16 per-group zeros y_ptr, # [N] bf16 output K, N, # dimensions BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) # Process K in groups ngroups = K // GROUP for g in range(ngroups): scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + g * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) k_base = g * GROUP # Within this group, process pairs of K elements (packed int4) for j in range(0, GROUP, 2): k_even = k_base + j x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + k_even).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + k_even + 1).to(tl.float32) packed_row = k_even // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * N + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) w_even = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) acc += x_even * (w_even - zero) * scale acc += x_odd * (w_odd - zero) * scale tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue: with Triton, loading from `wq_ptr + packed_row * N + n_offs` where `packed_row * N` uses a non-constexpr value might not work well with Triton's pointer analysis. Actually, Triton supports dynamic offsets via `tl.load` + pointer arithmetic, so this should work. But I should double-check: does Triton support uint8 loads and bitwise operations? In Triton 3.6.0, yes. One more consideration: the x_ptr loads are redundant across programs. Each program loads x_even and x_odd individually. With potentially 128 programs, each loading 2304 elements of x, that's 128 × 2304 = 295k loads, all loading the same data. But x is only 4.6KB and will be in L1 cache after the first access, so this should be fine. Actually, I realize there might be a performance issue with the inner loop. The inner loop iterates `group_size/2 = 64` times per group, and each iteration does 2 tl.load calls for x and 1 for w_q, plus unpack ops and FMAs. With 18 groups (for K=2304), that's 18 × 64 = 1152 iterations of the inner loop per program. Each iteration has several operations, but the key path is the w_q load which is coalesced and should hit L2 cache effectively. Let me also think about using a different kernel for different K sizes. For very small K (like kv_b where K=512, only 4 groups), the overhead of launching many programs might be high. For K=512, N=8192, with BLOCK_N=64, we have 128 programs. Each program does 4 groups × 64 iterations = 256 inner loop iterations. That's about 256 × (2 x loads + 1 wq load + compute). The overhead per program is low. For larger K (like the o_proj where K=4096), we have 32 groups × 64 iterations = 2048 inner loop iterations. Each program handles BLOCK_N=64 outputs, so with N=2304, we have 36 programs. I think this kernel design is solid. Let me now code it up properly. For the QuantLinear class, I'll create a wrapper that calls the fused kernel: ```python class FusedQuantLinear(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_f, out_f, group=128): super().__init__() self.in_f, self.out_f, self.group = in_f, out_f, group self.register_buffer("w_q", torch.zeros(in_f//2, out_f, dtype=torch.uint8)) self.register_buffer("scales", torch.zeros(in_f//group, out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16)) self.register_buffer("zeros", torch.zeros(in_f//group, out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16)) def forward(self, x): # x: [in_f] bf16 y = torch.empty(self.out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=x.device) grid = lambda meta: (triton.cdiv(self.out_f, meta['BLOCK_N']),) fused_int4_gemv_kernel[grid](x, self.w_q, self.scales, self.zeros, y, self.in_f, self.out_f, BLOCK_N=64, GROUP=128) return y ``` But wait, I need to think about the `step` function interface. In the reference, `QuantLinear.forward` is called as `self.q_proj(x)` where x is [hidden] bf16. I need my FusedQuantLinear to match this interface. Also, I need to make sure the `w_q`, `scales`, `zeros` buffer names match exactly so that `load_state_dict` works. The reference uses: - `w_q` (uint8) - `scales` (bf16) - `zeros` (bf16) These are the same names I'll use in my `FusedQuantLinear`. For `QuantExperts`, the reference stores: - `w_q` [E, K//2, N] uint8 - `scales` [E, K//group, N] bf16 - `zeros` [E, K//group, N] bf16 My version needs to match these names too. Let me now think about the QuantExperts forward. For the MoE, I need to do GEMV for multiple selected experts. I can either: 1. Loop over experts and call the fused GEMV kernel for each 2. Write a batched kernel Option 1 is simpler and should work fine. Let me go with that. For the MoE, the expert selection is: topk(router(x), k=8). Then for each selected expert: ``` h_gate = fused_gemv(x, expert_gate_weights[e]) h_up = fused_gemv(x, expert_up_weights[e]) h = silu(h_gate) * h_up out_e = fused_gemv(h, expert_down_weights[e]) ``` And for the shared expert: ``` h_gate_s = fused_gemv(x, shared_gate_weights[0]) h_up_s = fused_gemv(x, shared_up_weights[0]) h_s = silu(h_gate_s) * h_up_s out_s = fused_gemv(h_s, shared_down_weights[0]) ``` Output = sum(w[e] * out_e) + out_s For the shared expert, there's only 1 expert, so we can just directly index. Now, let me think about the MoE routing weights. The baseline uses `routed_scaling = 2.446`. The routing is: ``` probs = softmax(router(x)) w, idx = topk(probs, k=8) w = w / sum(w) * routed_scaling ``` Then each expert's output is weighted by w[j]. I'll write solution.py. One more thing: for the MLA absorb trick, I need to handle the per-head weight slicing. I'll add `N_stride` and `col_offset` parameters: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, N_stride, # stride between rows in weight buffer (can be > N for slicing) BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) ngroups = K // GROUP for g in range(ngroups): scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + g * N_stride + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g * N_stride + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) k_base = g * GROUP for j in range(0, GROUP, 2): k_even = k_base + j x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + k_even).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + k_even + 1).to(tl.float32) packed_row = k_even // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * N_stride + n_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) w_even = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) acc += x_even * (w_even - zero) * scale acc += x_odd * (w_odd - zero) * scale tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Then for the per-head case, I'd compute: ```python # W_UK for head h: columns h*128:(h+1)*128 wq_ptr = kv_b_wq.data_ptr() + h * 128 * kv_b_wq.element_size() # wait, wq is uint8 with shape [512//2, 8192] ``` No wait, the weight layout matters. `w_q` has shape [K//2, N] = [256, 8192] for kv_b. In PyTorch, the memory layout is row-major, so row `i` occupies `N` elements. To access columns `h*128:(h+1)*128`, I need to offset by `h*128` elements of size 1 byte each. For scales and zeros, shape is [K//128, N] = [4, 8192], bf16. Offset by `h*128 * 2` bytes. OK let me just code this properly. The kernel takes `n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)` which accesses columns `[col_offset + pid*BLOCK_N, col_offset + pid*BLOCK_N + BLOCK_N)`. Wait, I need to add col_offset to the pointer, not to n_offs. Let me restructure. Actually, the cleanest way is to pass already-offset pointers to the kernel. In PyTorch: ```python # kv_b weight: w_q [256, 8192] uint8, scales [4, 8192] bf16, zeros [4, 8192] bf16 # For head h, W_UK slice is columns [h*128 : (h+1)*128] wq_head = kv_b.w_q[:, h*128:(h+1)*128].contiguous() # [256, 128] scales_head = kv_b.scales[:, h*128:(h+1)*128].contiguous() # [4, 128] zeros_head = kv_b.zeros[:, h*128:(h+1)*128].contiguous() # [4, 128] ``` But `.contiguous()` creates a copy! That's expensive if we do it for all 32 heads. Better: use `as_strided` or just pass the base tensor with appropriate stride. But Triton doesn't understand PyTorch strides - it just sees raw pointers. Actually, Triton takes raw data pointers. If I pass: ```python wq_ptr = kv_b.w_q.data_ptr() + h * 128 # uint8, so + h*128 bytes scales_ptr = kv_b.scales.data_ptr() + h * 128 * 2 # bf16, so + h*128*2 bytes ``` And set N = 128, N_stride = 8192 (the full row stride), then the kernel will load rows correctly but only for the columns we care about. The `n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)` accesses columns `0..BLOCK_N-1` in the head-local space, which maps to columns `h*128..h*128+BLOCK_N-1` in the full weight space. So the kernel with N_stride would: - For row `packed_row`, load from `wq_ptr + packed_row * N_stride + n_offs` - Where `wq_ptr` already points to column `h*128` of the full weight - And `N_stride = 8192` (the stride between rows in the full weight) - So it loads `full_weight[packed_row, h*128 + n_offs]` ✓ This works! Let me use this approach. Similarly for scales/zeros: - `scales_ptr = kv_b.scales.data_ptr() + h * 128 * 2` points to column `h*128` - Kernel loads `scales_ptr + g * N_stride + n_offs * 2` which maps to `full_scales[g, h*128 + n_offs]` Wait, I need to be more careful. In the kernel, I do `tl.load(scales_ptr + g * N_stride + n_offs)`. Since scales_ptr is a pointer to bf16, the offset `g * N_stride + n_offs` is in units of bf16 elements. But N_stride is 8192 (the full number of columns). So `g * 8192 + n_offs` gives us element `g * 8192 + n_offs` from the base pointer that already points to column `h*128`. Total: element `g * 8192 + h*128 + n_offs`. ✓ And for wq_ptr: `packed_row * N_stride + n_offs` where wq_ptr is uint8, N_stride = 8192. So it accesses byte `packed_row * 8192 + n_offs` from pointer that points to column `h*128` in uint8 space. Total: byte `packed_row * 8192 + h*128 + n_offs`. ✓ Wait, w_q has shape [K//2, N]. Row `packed_row` occupies `N = 8192` bytes. So `packed_row * 8192 + (h*128 + n_offs)` is the correct byte offset. ✓ OK, this approach works. Let me proceed. Now, for the W_UV slice (v_head part): - Columns: H*qk_nope + h*v_head to H*qk_nope + (h+1)*v_head - = 4096 + h*128 to 4096 + (h+1)*128 - Offset in w_q: 4096 bytes (since uint8) - Offset in scales/zeros: 4096 * 2 bytes (since bf16) OK, I think I have a solid plan. Let me now write the code. One more optimization: for the KDA qkv projections, we can fuse them into a single kernel. The q, k, v projections all take the same input x and have the same weight dimensions (2304, 4096). We can compute all three in one pass: ```python y = fused_qkv_gemv(x, w_qkv, scales_qkv, zeros_qkv) # y has shape [3 * H * Dk] = [12288] # Split into q, k, v q, k, v = y.split(H * Dk) ``` This saves reading x three times. Update cache st["c_kv"] = torch.cat([st["c_kv"], c_kv_new[None]], 0) st["k_rope"] = torch.cat([st["k_rope"], k_rope_new[None]], 0) # --- ABSORB TRICK --- # Compute q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T for each head q_absorb = torch.empty(H, self.cfg.kv_lora, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=x.device) for h in range(H): # bf16 offset zeros_ptr = self.kv_b.zeros.data_ptr() + col_off * 2 # q_nope[h] is [128], W_UK[h]^T is [128, 512] # GEMV: y = q_nope[h] @ transpose(W_UK[h]) # Our kernel: y[n] = sum_k x[k] * w[k, n] where w is [K, N_stored] # = [512] # So q_absorb[h][j] = sum_i q_nope[h,i] * W_UK[h][j,i] # = sum_i q_nope[h,i] * weight[j, col_off + i] # # Kernel: y[n] = sum_k x[k] * w[k, n] # Match: y = q_absorb[h], x = q_nope[h], w[k,n] = weight[n, col_off + k] # For each k, it loads w[k, n_offs:n_offs+BLOCK_N]. # w is stored as [K, N_out] where N_out is the output dimension. # # For the q_absorb computation: # q_absorb[h][j] = sum_i q_nope[h,i] * weight[j, col_off + i] # x[i] = q_nope[h,i] # We need w[i, j] = weight[j, col_off + i] # # So K = 128 (qk_nope, the reduction dimension) # N = 512 (kv_lora, the output dimension) # w[i, j] = weight[j, col_off + i] # # w[q] is packed: w_q[i//2, j] contains weight[2*(i//2), col_off+j] and weight[2*(i//2)+1, col_off+j] # But we need weight[j, col_off + i]. This is NOT how the weight is packed! # The weight is packed along the first dimension (K=512), not along the second. # So w_q is [512//2, 8192] = [256, 8192]. # # Our kernel assumes w_q is [K//2, N_out] where K is the reduction dim. # For q_absorb, K=128 (qk_nope), N_out=512 (kv_lora). # But w_q is [256, 8192] in the original weight layout. # We need w_q that gives us: w_q[i//2, j] contains the packed values for weight[j, col_off + i] # But the original w_q is: w_q[j//2, col_off + i] = packed weight[j, col_off + i] and weight[j+1, col_off + i] # # The issue is that the "K" dimension in our kernel maps to different physical dimensions # in the weight storage. Our kernel assumes K is the packed dimension, but here K=128 # maps to the qk_nope dimension of the output, NOT the packed dimension. # # So I need to either: # 1. Transpose the weight before passing to the kernel # 2. Write a kernel that handles this transposed access pattern # Option 2 is better. Let me write a different kernel for this case. ``` OK wait, I'm overcomplicating this. Let me step back and think about what the kernel needs to do. For the q_absorb computation, I need: q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T Where: - q_nope[h] is [128] bf16 - W_UK[h] is [512, 128] (kv_lora × qk_nope), a slice of the kv_b weight - q_absorb[h] is [512] bf16 More explicitly: q_absorb[h][j] = sum_{i=0}^{127} q_nope[h][i] * weight[j, h*128 + i] This is a GEMV where the weight matrix is W_UK[h] with shape [512, 128], and we're computing x @ W^T where x has shape [128]. Our current kernel computes y = x @ W where W has shape [K, N] = [in, out]. For this case: - x = q_nope[h], shape [128] - W = W_UK[h]^T, shape [128, 512] - y = q_absorb[h], shape [512] So K = 128, N = 512, and the weight data is the transpose of the stored slice. The stored weight (kv_b weight) has shape [512, 8192] with columns col_off:(col_off+128) for W_UK[h]. W_UK[h][j, i] = weight[j, col_off + i] for j in [0,512), i in [0,128) W_UK[h]^T[i, j] = weight[j, col_off + i] So for our kernel: w[i, j] = weight[j, col_off + i], where w is [128, 512]. But the kernel loads: wq_ptr[packed_row * N_stride + n_offs], where packed_row = i//2, n_offs = j..j+BLOCK_N We need: wq_ptr[i//2 * N_stride + j] = packed_weight[j, col_off + i] But the stored packed weight is: stored_wq[j//2, col_off + i] = pack(weight[j, col_off+i], weight[j+1, col_off+i]) That's packed along the first dimension (kv_lora), NOT along the second (qk_nope). So the packing doesn't align with our kernel's assumption about the K dimension. To handle this correctly, I need a different kernel for the transposed access pattern. Let me write a kernel specifically for the absorbed MLA case. Actually, let me think about this differently. Instead of writing a new kernel, I can transpose the weight slice in memory. The W_UK[h] slice is [512, 128], which transposed is [128, 512]. The transpose is 128*512*2 = 128KB per head, times 32 heads = 4MB total. That's a significant amount of memory but manageable. Actually, for each decode step, we only need to compute this once (not per token in the attention loop), so the cost is amortized. But wait, the W_UK[h] and W_UV[h] slices are FIXED (the weight doesn't change). I can precompute the transposed views once when the model is loaded, not on every step! Hmm, but the weight is quantized (int4). I can't just transpose the quantized weight - I'd need to dequant first, which defeats the purpose. Alternative: I can store transposed pointers. For each head, I create a view of the weight with the appropriate column offset. The kernel just needs to handle the fact that the "packing dimension" is along kv_lora (512), not along qk_nope (128). Let me write a kernel for this specific case. The kernel would: - Accept K_reduce = 128 (qk_nope), N_out = 512 (kv_lora) - But the weight is packed along N_out (512), not K_reduce (128) - So the kernel iterates over K_reduce, and for each k, loads a single weight element (not a packed pair) for all N_out outputs Wait, the weight elements are still packed in pairs! It's just that the pairs are along the N_out dimension (512), not the K_reduce dimension (128). So for a given k (which indexes into qk_nope), and for output n (which indexes into kv_lora), the weight is: weight[n, col_off + k] And the packed storage has: w_q[n//2, col_off + k] = pack(weight[n, col_off + k], weight[n+1, col_off + k]) So for our kernel, when iterating over k (0 to 127): - For each k, load a VECTOR of N_out=512 output values - But the values are packed in pairs along N_out: w_q[n//2, col_off+k] This means: for a fixed k, output neurons n and n+1 are packed together! Our kernel would need to process n in pairs and unpack them. Let me write this kernel: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_transposed_kernel( x_ptr, # [K_reduce] bf16 input wq_ptr, # [N_out//2, K_reduce] uint8 - note the transposed layout! scales_ptr, # [N_out//128, K_reduce] bf16 zeros_ptr, # [N_out//128, K_reduce] bf16 y_ptr, # [N_out] bf16 output K_reduce, # 128 (qk_nope) N_out, # 512 (kv_lora) BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP: tl.constexpr, ): """ GEMV where the weight is a column slice of the full weight matrix: y[n] = sum_k x[k] * W_original[n, col_off + k] The weight is packed along N_out (the first dimension of W_original). w_q has shape [N_out//2, K_reduce] where: - Row n//2, column k: low nibble = W_original[n, col_off+k], high = W_original[n+1, col_off+k] """ pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N_out acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) # Iterate over K_reduce (the reduction dimension, qk_nope) for k in range(K_reduce): x_k = tl.load(x_ptr + k).to(tl.float32) # The weight is packed along N_out, so we need to process n in pairs for j in range(0, BLOCK_N, 2): n_abs = pid * BLOCK_N + j if n_abs >= N_out: break # Load packed byte for (n_abs, n_abs+1) pair at column k packed_row = n_abs // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * K_reduce + k) w_lo = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_hi = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Load scale/zero for this group # Groups are along N_out: group size 128 grp_idx = n_abs // GROUP # Hmm, the scale/zero layout... # scales has shape [N_out//128, K_reduce] = [4, 128] per head # scales[grp_idx, k] is the scale for group grp_idx at position k ``` Hmm wait, this is getting messy because of the group structure. Let me reconsider. Actually, the group size is 128 along the INPUT dimension of the weight, which is kv_lora = 512. But kv_lora is the OUTPUT dimension of the transposed GEMV. So groups are along the wrong dimension for this access pattern. I think the simplest approach for the absorb trick is: DON'T use the grouped dequant structure for per-head slices. Instead, I'll dequant the full kv_b weight ONCE and then use regular bf16 GEMV with the dequantized weight slices. The full kv_b weight dequantized is [512, 8192] bf16 = 8MB. The baseline does this anyway. The absorb trick saves the later kv_b GEMV with the KV cache (which grows with context). The initial kv_b dequant to bf16 is a fixed cost independent of context length. Wait, but 8MB of dequantized weight negates the bandwidth advantage of int4 weights. Let me think about this trade-off: Option A: Dequant kv_b once (read int4 2MB + write bf16 8MB = 10MB). Then use bf16 weight slices for absorb (read 8MB per step). Total per step: 10MB (one-time?) + ctx_dependent traffic. Option B: Fuse the dequant into per-head GEMVs (no full dequant). Total per step: read int4 weight slices (2MB) + ctx_dependent traffic. Option B saves 8MB per step vs Option A (assuming the 8MB bf16 weight is re-read each step, which it might not be if cached). But Option B is more complex. Actually, 8MB is in the range of L2 cache on the RTX PRO 6000 (which has 96MB L2). So the dequantized kv_b weight might stay in L2 across steps, making the additional cost near zero. Hmm, but the int4 approach (Option B) would use 2MB in L2 instead of 8MB, leaving more room for other data. Given the large KV cache at long context lengths, this matters. OK, let me go with a custom kernel for the absorb case. Let me think about this more carefully. The key insight: for the absorbed GEMV, I need to access the weight matrix in a transposed way. The stored weight has shape [kv_lora=512, total_dim=8192] with packing along kv_lora. But for the per-head GEMV, I need to access a column slice and treat kv_lora as the output dimension. For q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T: - W_UK[h] is columns [h*128, (h+1)*128] of the full weight [512, 8192] - q_nope[h] is [128] - q_absorb[h][j] = sum_{i=0}^{127} q_nope[h,i] * weight[j, h*128+i] for j=0..511 So for a given output neuron j (indexing kv_lora) and a given reduction index i (indexing qk_nope), the weight is weight[j, h*128+i]. The weight is packed along j (kv_lora), so: w_q[j//2, h*128+i] contains the packed low nibble for weight[j, h*128+i] and high nibble for weight[j+1, h*128+i]. In the kernel, I iterate over i (reduction dim, 0..127): - For each i, load x[i] and weight[:, h*128+i] - weight[:, col] gives us 512 values, packed as 256 bytes - For each pair (j, j+1), unpack and accumulate This means: for each i, I load 256 bytes of weight and process them. That's 128 × 256 = 32KB of weight reads per head × 32 heads = 1MB total. Very efficient! Here's the kernel: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_colslice_kernel( x_ptr, # [K_reduce] bf16 — q_nope[h] wq_ptr, # pointer to w_q[:, col_start] — shape in memory is [N_out//2, K_reduce] # Actually w_q has shape [N_out//2, total_N], and we point to col_start wq_stride, # stride between rows of w_q (total_N, not K_reduce) scales_ptr, # pointer to scales[:, col_start] scales_stride, # stride between rows (total_N) zeros_ptr, # pointer to zeros[:, col_start] zeros_stride, # stride between rows (total_N) y_ptr, # [N_out] bf16 K_reduce, # 128 (qk_nope) N_out, # 512 (kv_lora) BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N_out acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) # Iterate over reduction dimension K_reduce for k in range(K_reduce): x_k = tl.load(x_ptr + k).to(tl.float32) # Load weight column k for output neurons n_offs # Process in pairs since weight is packed along N_out for j in range(0, BLOCK_N, 2): n_abs = pid * BLOCK_N + j if n_abs >= N_out: break packed_row = n_abs // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * wq_stride + k) w_lo = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Get scale/zero for this group # Groups are along the storage (kv_lora) dimension, group_size=128 g = n_abs // GROUP_SIZE scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + g * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g * zeros_stride + k).to(tl.float32) # Accumulate for even neuron acc_j = x_k * (w_lo - zero) * scale # Use atomic-like accumulation into acc[j] # In Triton, we can just index: acc[j] += ... if n_abs + 1 < N_out: w_hi = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) g2 = (n_abs + 1) // GROUP_SIZE scale2 = tl.load(scales_ptr + g2 * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) if g2 != g else scale zero2 = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g2 * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) if g2 != g else zero # accumulate for odd neuron ``` Wait, there's a problem. In Triton, I can't easily do `acc[j] += ...` where j is a loop variable. Let me restructure. Actually, in Triton, I can accumulate into individual elements of a vector using regular indexing if the index is a `tl.constexpr` or can be resolved at compile time. But `j` is a loop variable... Let me restructure the kernel to process one pair at a time inline, without a loop: Actually, for a simpler approach, I can set BLOCK_N=2 and have each program handle exactly one pair of output neurons. This is wasteful for GPU utilization but simpler. Or, I can set BLOCK_N to be a power of 2 and handle all pairs within it: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_colslice_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, wq_stride, scales_ptr, scales_stride, zeros_ptr, zeros_stride, y_ptr, K_reduce, N_out, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N n_offs = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N_out acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) for k in range(K_reduce): x_k = tl.load(x_ptr + k).to(tl.float32) # We need to handle even/odd pairs here # For each pair of adjacent output neurons, they share a packed byte for j in range(0, BLOCK_N, 2): # Check if both neurons are valid n0 = n_start + j n1 = n_start + j + 1 if n0 < N_out: packed_row = n0 // 2 wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * wq_stride + k) lo = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) g0 = n0 // GROUP_SIZE s0 = tl.load(scales_ptr + g0 * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) z0 = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g0 * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) # acc[j] += x_k * (lo - z0) * s0 # But we can't index into acc with j (Python loop variable) in Triton ``` Hmm, Triton doesn't support dynamic indexing into its vector registers in this way. The standard pattern is to use `tl.where` or to unroll the loop. Let me take a different approach. Instead of a loop over pairs, let me process all pairs via masking: Actually, I think a much cleaner approach is to make the kernel 1D over output neurons but handle the fact that weights are packed in pairs. Since BLOCK_N is a compile-time constant, I can unroll: Actually, the simplest approach is to just use BLOCK_N and do all-neuron processing. Instead of trying to handle pairs inside a loop, let me process all neurons at once using masks to separate even and odd neurons: ```python # Even neurons: n_offs = 0, 2, 4, ..., BLOCK_N-2 # Odd neurons: n_offs = 1, 3, 5, ..., BLOCK_N-1 # For a given k, load wq bytes for all even rows # Each byte contains (w[even], w[odd]) # For BLOCK_N=64, we need 32 bytes ``` This is getting complicated. Let me take a step back and think about whether I really need the absorb trick to win. If I just fuse the int4 dequant into all GEMVs WITHOUT the absorb trick, vs baseline: - Baseline: dequant weight to bf16 (read int4 + write bf16), then matmul (read bf16) - Fused: read int4 only, compute matmul in one pass The speedup from fused int4 GEMV alone should be: - Read int4: 1/4 of bf16 bytes - No bf16 write - Same compute For bandwidth-bound decode, saving 75% of weight bandwidth should give ~2-3x speedup. The absorb trick adds more on top, especially at long context, but is not essential for a meaningful speedup. Let me implement the fused int4 GEMV first, verify correctness, then consider adding the absorb trick if there's time. Let me revisit the absorb trick with a clean implementation. Let me think about this from scratch. For the MLA absorb trick, the key operations are: 1. q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T for h=0..31 - q_nope[h]: [128] bf16 - W_UK[h]: slice of kv_b weight, shape [512, 128] - Result: [512] bf16 2. scores[h, t] = q_absorb[h] @ c_kv[t] + q_rope[h] @ k_rope[t] - Standard attention scores, computed in fp32 3. c_kv_weighted[h] = sum_t softmax(scores[h])[t] * c_kv[t] - Weighted sum of KV latents, result [512] per head 4. o_absorb[h] = c_kv_weighted[h] @ W_UV[h] for h=0..31 - c_kv_weighted[h]: [512] bf16 - W_UV[h]: slice of kv_b weight, shape [512, 128] - Result: [128] bf16 For steps 1 and 4, I need GEMVs with column slices of the kv_b weight. Each such GEMV is between a [128] or [512] vector and a [512, 128] weight slice. Let me just write these GEMVs using a carefully designed kernel. The key complication is that the kv_b weight is packed along the 512 dimension (kv_lora), but for step 1, the reduction dimension is 128 (qk_nope), and for step 4, the reduction dimension is 512 (kv_lora). These need different access patterns! For step 1 (q_absorb): - Reduction along k=0..127 (qk_nope dimension) - Output along j=0..511 (kv_lora dimension) - Weight[j, k] = kv_b_full[j, h*128 + k] - Packed: w_q[j//2, h*128 + k] = pack(weight[j, h*128+k], weight[j+1, h*128+k]) For step 4 (o_absorb): - Reduction along k=0..511 (kv_lora dimension) - Output along j=0..127 (v_head dimension) - Weight[k, j] = kv_b_full[k, H*qk_nope + h*128 + j] - Packed: w_q[k//2, H*qk_nope + h*128 + j] = pack(weight[k, H*128+h*128+j], weight[k+1, H*128+h*128+j]) For step 4, the weight access pattern matches our standard fused GEMV kernel! The reduction dim (k=kv_lora) IS the packed dimension. So our standard kernel works for step 4. For step 1, the reduction dim (k=qk_nope) is NOT the packed dimension. But k is only 128 elements. We can write a specialized kernel for this. Alternatively, for step 1, I can just dequant the specific weight slice and do a regular bf16 GEMV. The slice is [512, 128] bf16 = 128KB per head. With 32 heads, that's 4MB total if I do all heads at once. But I only need one head at a time. Dequantizing the slice costs: read int4 weight (512//2 * 128 = 32KB), write bf16 (512 * 128 * 2 = 128KB). The dequant is a fixed cost per step, not per context token. Actually, for step 1, q_nope[h] is only 128 elements long. The GEMV q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T does 128 × 512 = 65K multiply-adds. This is TINY. The overhead of launching a Triton kernel here is significant compared to the computation. For such small operations, using PyTorch with dequantized weight slices is actually fine. The weights for one head are only 128KB in bf16. Let me dequant just the needed slices. Wait, but the prompt says: "The job is to fuse the int4 unpack and per-group dequant directly into the GEMV so you stream the int4 weights once." And also: "do not import a prebuilt quantized kernel." I think the spirit is: fuse dequant+GEMV for the BIG weight matrices. The per-head kv_b slices are small (128 columns out of 8192). Fusing them is nice but not critical. Let me write a specialized kernel for step 1. Actually, let me just write a clean solution. For step 1: ```python # For each head h: # W_UK[h] = kv_b weight slice [512, 128] at columns [h*128:(h+1)*128] # q_absorb[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_UK[h]^T # The weight is packed along dim 0 (kv_lora=512). # q_nope[h] has shape [128]. # W_UK[h]^T has shape [128, 512]. # q_absorb[h][j] = sum_i q_nope[h,i] * weight[j, h*128+i] # In the kernel, iterate over i (0..127): # For each i, we need weight[:, col_off+i] = 512 values packed as 256 bytes # This is a column of the weight matrix at column col_off+i # Write a kernel that processes a slice of output neurons (BLOCK_N at a time) # For each k in range(K_reduce): # x_k = x[k] # Load column k of weight for this block of output neurons # Since weight is packed along the output (kv_lora) dim, each byte # in the column packs two adjacent output neurons ``` OK let me just write this kernel. I'll iterate over the reduction dim (qk_nope) and for each iteration, handle the packed output neurons. ```python @triton.jit def int4_gemv_slice_kernel( x_ptr, # [K_red] bf16 wq_ptr, # uint8 weight, shape [N_out//2, total_cols], pointing to col_start wq_stride, # total_cols (for row stride) scales_ptr, # bf16, pointing to col_start scales_stride, # total_cols zeros_ptr, # bf16 zeros_stride, # total_cols y_ptr, # [N_out] bf16 K_red, # reduction dimension (128 for q_absorb) N_out, # output dimension (512 for q_absorb) BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N n_offs_even = n_start + 2 * tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N // 2) # even indices n_offs_odd = n_offs_even + 1 # odd indices # Mask for valid output indices mask_even = n_offs_even < N_out mask_odd = n_offs_odd < N_out acc_even = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N // 2,), dtype=tl.float32) acc_odd = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N // 2,), dtype=tl.float32) for k in range(K_red): x_k = tl.load(x_ptr + k).to(tl.float32) # Load weight byte for each even output neuron # w_q[even//2, col_off + k] contains the packed byte packed_rows = n_offs_even // 2 # = n_start//2 + tl.arange(...) wq_bytes = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_rows * wq_stride + k, mask=mask_even, other=0) # Unpack w_lo = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # weight for even neurons w_hi = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # weight for odd neurons # Load scales/zeros for each neuron's group grp_even = n_offs_even // GROUP grp_odd = n_offs_odd // GROUP s_even = tl.load(scales_ptr + grp_even * scales_stride + k, mask=mask_even, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) z_even = tl.load(zeros_ptr + grp_even * scales_stride + k, mask=mask_even, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) s_odd = tl.load(scales_ptr + grp_odd * scales_stride + k, mask=mask_odd, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) z_odd = tl.load(zeros_ptr + grp_odd * scales_stride + k, mask=mask_odd, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Accumulate acc_even += x_k * (w_lo - z_even) * s_even acc_odd += x_k * (w_hi - z_odd) * s_odd # Store results interleaved: even, odd, even, odd, ... # We need to store back to y in order y_even = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N, 2) y_odd = tl.arange(1, BLOCK_N, 2) # Hmm, we can't easily interleave in a single store # Alternative: two stores tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs_even, acc_even.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_even) tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs_odd, acc_odd.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_odd) ``` This is getting messy. Let me try a different, much cleaner approach. Actually, the SIMPLEST clean approach is to make BLOCK_N process TWO output neurons (a pair), which naturally aligns with the packing: ```python @triton.jit def int4_gemv_slice_pair_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, wq_stride, scales_ptr, scales_stride, zeros_ptr, zeros_stride, y_ptr, K_red, N_out, GROUP: tl.constexpr, ): """ Each program processes 2 output neurons (since they share a packed byte). """ pid = tl.program_id(0) n_pair = pid # pair index, processes neurons 2*pid and 2*pid+1 if n_pair * 2 >= N_out: return n_even = n_pair * 2 n_odd = n_even + 1 acc_even = tl.zeros((1,), dtype=tl.float32) acc_odd = tl.zeros((1,), dtype=tl.float32) # Pre-compute group indices g_even = n_even // GROUP g_odd = n_odd // GROUP if n_odd < N_out else g_even for k in range(K_red): x_k = tl.load(x_ptr + k).to(tl.float32) # Load packed byte packed_row = n_even // 2 # = n_pair wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + packed_row * wq_stride + k) w_lo = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) s_even = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_even * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) z_even = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_even * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) acc_even += x_k * (w_lo - z_even) * s_even if n_odd < N_out: w_hi = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) s_odd = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_odd * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) z_odd = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_odd * scales_stride + k).to(tl.float32) acc_odd += x_k * (w_hi - z_odd) * s_odd tl.store(y_ptr + n_even, acc_even.to(tl.bfloat16)) if n_odd < N_out: tl.store(y_ptr + n_odd, acc_odd.to(tl.bfloat16)) ``` This is clean! Each program handles a pair of output neurons. For N_out=512, we need 256 programs. Each program iterates over K_red=128, does the loads and computes. For step 4 (o_absorb), the standard fused GEMV kernel works since the reduction dim IS the packed dim. But the weights are a column slice of kv_b. Actually, I just realized: I can also write a fused kernel for kv_b that does BOTH the dequant AND the per-head GEMV in one kernel, for all heads. This would be the most efficient approach. Let me write a single Triton kernel that computes q_absorb for all 32 heads: ```python @triton.jit def mla_q_absorb_kernel( q_nope_ptr, # [32, 128] bf16 (all heads) wq_ptr, # [256, 8192] uint8 (kv_b weight) scales_ptr, # [4, 8192] bf16 zeros_ptr, # [4, 8192] bf16 y_ptr, # [32, 512] bf16 output qk_nope: tl.constexpr, # 128 kv_lora: tl.constexpr, # 512 total_N: tl.constexpr, # 8192 GROUP: tl.constexpr, # 128 ): """ Computes q_absorb[h, j] = sum_i q_nope[h, i] * kv_b[j, h*qk_nope + i] for all heads h and output positions j. Grid: (32 heads, ceil(kv_lora/2)) Each program handles 2 output neurons for one head (one packed byte). """ h = tl.program_id(0) # head index pair_idx = tl.program_id(1) # pair index in output n_even = pair_idx * 2 n_odd = n_even + 1 if n_even >= kv_lora: return # Column offset for this head col_off = h * qk_nope # = h * 128 # Pre-compute group indices g_even = n_even // GROUP g_odd = n_odd // GROUP if n_odd < kv_lora else g_even acc_even = 0.0 acc_odd = 0.0 for i in range(qk_nope): # Load q_nope[h, i] q_val = tl.load(q_nope_ptr + h * qk_nope + i).to(tl.float32) # Load packed byte: w_q[n_even//2, col_off + i] = w_q[pair_idx, col_off + i] wq_byte = tl.load(wq_ptr + pair_idx * total_N + col_off + i) w_lo = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) s_even = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_even * total_N + col_off + i).to(tl.float32) z_even = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_even * total_N + col_off + i).to(tl.float32) acc_even += q_val * (w_lo - z_even) * s_even if n_odd < kv_lora: w_hi = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) s_odd = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_odd * total_N + col_off + i).to(tl.float32) z_odd = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_odd * total_N + col_off + i).to(tl.float32) acc_odd += q_val * (w_hi - z_odd) * s_odd # Store tl.store(y_ptr + h * kv_lora + n_even, acc_even.to(tl.bfloat16)) if n_odd < kv_lora: tl.store(y_ptr + h * kv_lora + n_odd, acc_odd.to(tl.bfloat16)) ``` This is clean!
